  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_546e7de7-0bf7-4e80-98f2-fb970e94d6b0'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1632583837601374926">A wild ride to the dark side of Americana The Curse of Lono is to Hawaii what Fear and Loathing was to Las Vegas: the crazy tales of a journalist's ""coverage"" of a news event that ends up being a wild ride to the dark side of Americana. Originally published in 1983, Curse features all of the zany, hallucinogenic wordplay and feral artwork for which the Hunter S. Thompson</span>
  <span id="freeText1632583837601374926" style="display:none">A wild ride to the dark side of Americana The Curse of Lono is to Hawaii what Fear and Loathing was to Las Vegas: the crazy tales of a journalist's ""coverage"" of a news event that ends up being a wild ride to the dark side of Americana. Originally published in 1983, Curse features all of the zany, hallucinogenic wordplay and feral artwork for which the Hunter S. Thompson/Ralph Steadman duo became known and loved. This curious book, considered an oddity among Hunter's oeuvre, was long out of print, prompting collectors to search high and low for an original copy. TASCHEN's signed, limited edition sold out before the book even hit the stores, but this unlimited version, in a different, smaller format, makes The Curse of Lono accessible to everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1632583837601374926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3610838778?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3610838778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6133302421648962586">I accepted my assignment with some wild trepidation. It’d been a while since I spent time with Dr. Hunter S. Thompson and I knew the crazy bastard would give me the shakes if I let off the throttle for half a second.<br/><br/>Before I uploaded the book, his 1983 visit to Hawaii and the dark underbelly of Americana, I fired up the El Camino and went for provisions, I wouldn’t be left stranded high and dry like a potbellied iguana the way I had in Costa Rica; I’d be ready this time. I surveyed my haul: thre</span>
  <span id="freeText6133302421648962586" style="display:none">I accepted my assignment with some wild trepidation. It’d been a while since I spent time with Dr. Hunter S. Thompson and I knew the crazy bastard would give me the shakes if I let off the throttle for half a second.<br><br>Before I uploaded the book, his 1983 visit to Hawaii and the dark underbelly of Americana, I fired up the El Camino and went for provisions, I wouldn’t be left stranded high and dry like a potbellied iguana the way I had in Costa Rica; I’d be ready this time. I surveyed my haul: three cases of Kona Beer, two gallons of rum, a couple pints of gin, a handle of Kentucky bourbon, a carton of American Spirit cigarettes and a bag of Lays potato chips. I was on a shoestring budget, but this might just get me through to screaming end.<br><br>Thompson was in rare form, describing a quick survey of the Hawaii marathon that ended up with weeks on the Kona shore, holed up like a gang of demented ferrets in a hot dog factory. The good doctor mixed in his cacophony of gonzo literature with snippets of history of the island and especially of Captain James Cook and his fatal last voyage and his attempted kidnapping of a Hawaiian king. It was a blood curdling song of despair, nervousness and confusion – but worth the shamble through the back alleys of Honolulu to a high resting place atop a summit of clear perspective.<br><br>So long again, Thompson, you frenzied maniac, we’ll meet again.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1605120221i/30365451._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="285" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_64608824" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64608824" />
    <a title="Christopherseelie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86397-christopherseelie"><img alt="Christopherseelie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1197531044p2/86397.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64608824?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopherseelie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopherseelie" href="/user/show/86397-christopherseelie">Christopherseelie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">HST fans, anyone up for a dark laugh</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64608824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1791748867841034718">The most elaborate, hilarious, and engrossing "big fish" tale ever committed to print. This is one of Hunter Thompson's greatest achievements, and it doesn't hurt that the packaging is lavish. A huge coffeetable book with quality prints, facsimiles of the Good Doctor's relevant letters, and interspersed excerpts from other books that fill in the Hawaiian history relevant to the story. What is so fascinating about this last feature is that it reveals what other HST books have left out: the studio</span>
  <span id="freeText1791748867841034718" style="display:none">The most elaborate, hilarious, and engrossing "big fish" tale ever committed to print. This is one of Hunter Thompson's greatest achievements, and it doesn't hurt that the packaging is lavish. A huge coffeetable book with quality prints, facsimiles of the Good Doctor's relevant letters, and interspersed excerpts from other books that fill in the Hawaiian history relevant to the story. What is so fascinating about this last feature is that it reveals what other HST books have left out: the studious preparation behind the wild stories.<br />Roughly ten years after Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas, the action opens with the line "We were about forty minutes out of San Fransisco when the crew finally decided to take action on the problem in Lavatory 1B." Remarkably similar to the iconic opening "We were somewhere around Barstow on the edge of the desert when the drugs began to take hold". And so begins a manifold tale of America in the '80s-a tale of a Christmas vacation gone horribly, horribly wrong-a tale of high stakes sports fishing-and the tale of a man finding his inner godliness on the westernmost edge of America.<br />Put that way, The Curse of Lono might be the most ambitious book in the Thompson oeuvre. Either way, I personally think it is his funniest.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1791748867841034718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169880645" />
    <a title="A" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3109313-a"><img alt="A" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

<div id="review_1657980779" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1657980779" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1657980779?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=ebook-special-coll">ebook-special-coll</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection">__in-my-collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=_lfpl-library">_lfpl-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=drugs-n-shit">drugs-n-shit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=epistle-larry-approves">epistle-larry-approves</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=hst">hst</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1657980779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14299618620747701649">One afternoon this week as I rode my bicycle home from the library I passed under the branches of an array of stout old trees along the roadway. But I had my suspicions about them, and this, more or less verbatim, was what I was thinking:<br/>"They look safe enough, but you read about their dastardly ways once in awhile, dropping a trunk onto some hapless unsuspecting bastard, pile-driving him into oblivion. You'd think I was safe, being a tree-hugger, but they don't care. I'm human."<br/><br/>Now, would I hav</span>
  <span id="freeText14299618620747701649" style="display:none">One afternoon this week as I rode my bicycle home from the library I passed under the branches of an array of stout old trees along the roadway. But I had my suspicions about them, and this, more or less verbatim, was what I was thinking:<br />"They look safe enough, but you read about their dastardly ways once in awhile, dropping a trunk onto some hapless unsuspecting bastard, pile-driving him into oblivion. You'd think I was safe, being a tree-hugger, but they don't care. I'm human."<br /><br />Now, would I have thought about that, in this way, unless I had read Hunter S. Thompson? I don't think so.<br /><br />The funny thing is, once you've read some Hunter, his mode of thinking becomes yours, or maybe that only happens with people who share some kindred spirit with him, with his skewed outlook; I was already inclined toward that bent. I'm also a native of Louisville, like Thompson, and that's an exclusive club of shared weirdness.<br /><br />My imitation is easily explained, too, because there is a certain formula to Thompson, and it attracts all manner of bad imitators. The fact that it can be replicated does not detract from its originality, and its particular qualities -- which can only be had from lived observation -- means his prose style can never be duplicated without someone calling the faker out.<br /><br /><i>The Curse of Lono</i> has long been considered a bastard stepchild in Thompson's <i>oeuvre</i>. For some reason, it was only given a small print run in 1983 after which its rarity ensured its cult status and high prices for used copies. Upon Thompson's death in 2005, it was reissued in a deluxe edition sporting even more of collaborator Ralph Steadman's beautifully twisted paintings and illustrations. That edition, too, became a collectible.<br /><br />Why the book has been treated as a specialty item is a mystery to me, because, at least in terms of laughs per square column inch, it possibly beats even <i>Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas</i>.<br /><br />As I started reading it, I was inclined to think of it as a junior league retread of <i>...Vegas</i>, down to its conceit of having a reporter, Thompson, covering a minor local sporting event (a marathon in Honolulu), then losing interest in it quickly and setting off on a maddening, aimless, drug-fueled surreal adventure informed of local lore and mythology.<br /><br />To a certain extent, Thompson does stumble into a well-gouged mine to extract some familiar ore. Luckily, though, he's very good at this and the story, though derivative, has its own grotesque charms as well as the always impish off-kilter humor that I love so much in Thompson's work, and as it proceeded, the book won me over. Even when the incidents to some degree seem like repeated variations on a theme, and the constant drug references threatened to take the wink-and-nudge factor too far, I was constantly impressed by Thompson's piquant observations and brutal cosmic punchlines.<br /><br />Once again, Hunter faces The Fear in search of The Wisdom, fearlessly and irrationally, or at least his first-person alter-ego protagonist does. In the process, he stares it down -- the locals facing off against the outsider, and vice versa. The result is ironic, comic gold.<br /><br />The book's "plot," in a nutshell, has Hunter and a drug-dealing acquaintance flying to Hawaii to cover the marathon, meeting there the writer's always reluctant artistic collaborator Steadman and his family. After aceing some beautiful and hilarious observations on the insanity of running, he and his cadre head for the Kona Coast on the big island where things get much stranger. There Thompson becomes determined to snare the ultimate prize, a big Blue Marlin, on a fishing trip journey that matches any harrowing sea adventure. During all of this, a hospital-load of drugs and booze are downed, Steadman becomes deathly ill causing him and his family to split and allowing Thompson to continue to charge insane amounts on his running tab, a fishing companion has his toe crushed by an oxygen tank, a firecracker bomb wreaks unintended havoc, the locals and beach thugs get very surly, the weather turns vicious, Thompson is stung in the eye by a wasp stuck behind his shades, a hibachi grill nearly threatens disaster on the sea-tossed fishing vessel, murderous and litigious real-estate moguls threaten vengeance, a stubborn anchor almost leaves the hapless fishing crew forever adrift, a companion has his armed died blue for the entire book as the result of an airplane toilet bowl mishap, a savage outbreak of red fleas infects the men's mascot, a major drug operation threatens to expose the motley crew, a veterinarian ships out illegal drugs labeled "dog medicine..." and so on. <br /><br />On top of all this, Thompson is pondering the island legend of Lono, the God who the locals once dubbed Captain Cook before cultural misunderstandings led to his violent death. When the crazed Thompson arrives in port with his proud marlin catch, he harbors a God complex and delusions of grandeur and proclaims himself to be the God Lono returned to them, a move that goes too far, pissing off the islanders. You can say and do almost anything, he is told, but don't mess with their religion. Needless to say, he goes into hiding with the help of a weed-smoking park ranger.<br /><br />The book's descriptions of sport fishing are great and the attendant tales imaginative. This is possibly the funniest fishing story ever written. <br /><br />And, trigger warning for the easily offended, the book is sprinkled with Thompson's typical casual racism, which one can deal with in context.<br /><br />The last chapters in the book take an epistolary form, in letters to Ralph Steadman, and conclude with a breathlessly beautiful sentence of Thompson describing a companion's swim in the sea.<br /><br />This is not a book the Hawaii Chamber of Commerce could ever endorse. This is not the Hawaii of blue skies, crystal seas and languid green palms. This is the alternate Hawaiian universe, a Dantesque version of a paradise as hell on earth. Leave it to Thompson to find that underbelly.<br /><br />And nowhere else would you find a friend's alienation described as "kinky brooding" -- an amazing insight into the contradictory impulses of self pity.<br /><br />I will admit, I read an online "renegade" copy of this, so I was not able to see all of Steadman's crazy and amazing illustrations, but most of those too are posted online to see.<br /><br />This was a unique experience and I would like to read it again someday.<br /><br />(Kr@Ky 2016)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14299618620747701649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arthur" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arthur" href="/user/show/6448877-arthur">Arthur</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer215301213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5170417164823220589">This is the Hunter Thompson book you've never heard of, but really want to read. It's mostly about how crappy Hawaii is. It has the best ending of any of his stuff I've read, hands down. <br/><br/>Getting the book though is another matter. The paperback of it has been out of print since the early 80s. It's only produced now by Taschen, the art book company, as a big 60 dollar coffee table book. And they only started really printing it about a year after the first batch of 1000. The first 1000 Taschen put </span>
  <span id="freeText5170417164823220589" style="display:none">This is the Hunter Thompson book you've never heard of, but really want to read. It's mostly about how crappy Hawaii is. It has the best ending of any of his stuff I've read, hands down. <br /><br />Getting the book though is another matter. The paperback of it has been out of print since the early 80s. It's only produced now by Taschen, the art book company, as a big 60 dollar coffee table book. And they only started really printing it about a year after the first batch of 1000. The first 1000 Taschen put out were signed by Ralph Steadman, Thompson's artist and partner, and numbered. You can find these first 1000 on used book pages on BN.com or Amazon for around a thousand bucks, making it one of the more pricey things I know of on those used services, besides that Madonna Sex book from the early 90s.<br /><br />So basically if you want to read it, you're screwed, unless of course you know somebody that has a DRM free digital copy. But who would possibly have one of those?</span>
  <a data-text-id="5170417164823220589" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer667515890" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer339743006759575330">My current favourite-book-of-all-time, from my current favourite-writer-of-all-time. <br/><br/>I never thought I'd ever get a chance to read this bad-boy, or at least, not until I hit the age of forty. But my parents were kind enough to splurge on it for my birthday back on July 11th, 2013. <br/><br/>I was pleasantly surprised to find that this thing is bloody ginormous. My version is a coffee-table book, meaning it could technically be used as a weapon or to club off intruders.<br/><br/>The pictures are beautiful, and the s</span>
  <span id="freeText339743006759575330" style="display:none">My current favourite-book-of-all-time, from my current favourite-writer-of-all-time. <br /><br />I never thought I'd ever get a chance to read this bad-boy, or at least, not until I hit the age of forty. But my parents were kind enough to splurge on it for my birthday back on July 11th, 2013. <br /><br />I was pleasantly surprised to find that this thing is bloody ginormous. My version is a coffee-table book, meaning it could technically be used as a weapon or to club off intruders.<br /><br />The pictures are beautiful, and the size of the book really allows them to come to life.<br /><br />The story itself is a wonderfully wacky Thompson tale which blends comedy and weirdness with darker stuff (the dogs, man!) and even some Hawaiian history. A genre-blender of the highest order.<br /><br />Highly recommended to anyone and everyone. I was laughing until tears came out of my eyes, and then two lines later I'd be questioning my own sanity.<br /><br />If only Hunter S. Thompson had written more...</span>
  <a data-text-id="339743006759575330" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Its a huge oversized hardback printed on high quality pages where Steadman's art really is given center stage. I keep it on display at all times!</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_284787730" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/284787730" />
    <a title="D.M." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1463719-d-m"><img alt="D.M." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220715186p2/1463719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/284787730?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D.M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="D.M." href="/user/show/1463719-d-m">D.M.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer284787730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15869518530109883982">Curse of Lono came out in 1983, which means I would've been about 14 when I found it inexplicable racked in my small town's sole bookshop/newsstand. I freely admit I'd never heard of Thompson or Steadman, and that it was actually the latter's frenetic and vaguely frightening art that drew me to the book. Ten bucks would've been hard for me to come by at the time (probably proceeds from my short-lived Sunday-paper route), but this book was something I had to have. It is not hyperbole to say that </span>
  <span id="freeText15869518530109883982" style="display:none">Curse of Lono came out in 1983, which means I would've been about 14 when I found it inexplicable racked in my small town's sole bookshop/newsstand. I freely admit I'd never heard of Thompson or Steadman, and that it was actually the latter's frenetic and vaguely frightening art that drew me to the book. Ten bucks would've been hard for me to come by at the time (probably proceeds from my short-lived Sunday-paper route), but this book was something I had to have. It is not hyperbole to say that it changed my life forever.<br />While now, in retrospect and having since read most of Thompson's writing, I can see this is not at all a classic book (let alone classic Thompson), when I was a small-town teenager it was a window in a world I couldn't have imagined existed. Most of my reading up to that point was no more recent than Sherlock Holmes, so to witness the savagery, catastrophe and balls-out insanity that was Hunter Thompson was mind-blowing.<br />The book today reads like what it was: Thompson attempting a return to classic form. By this time in his career, he had regrettably found himself utterly hemmed in by the Gonzo character he had created. In ...Lono, he admits he'd been turning down writing assignments for years when he took one to cover the Honolulu Marathon for a small mag called 'Running.' Perhaps he saw it as a chance to relive the magic that caused Fear And Loathing In Las Vegas (which, of course, started as an assignment to cover a motorcycle race, but turned into something bigger and stranger). Unfortunately, this book lacks the grand mission that ...Las Vegas took on. There is no American Dream pursued here, in fact nothing is pursued at all. Even what could have ended as a journey of self-discovery for Thompson ends in a manic fizzle rather than any kind of resolution. Too many threads are left dangling (Whatever happened to Ackerman and the pot? What became of the dog in the suite and its killer fleas?) to qualify this as a concrete novel either. What we end up with is a slightly beefed-up magazine article that reads like something between Rum Diary and ...Las Vegas, padded further by extensive excerpts from other sources on Hawaiian history (does he do that in ANY other book?).<br />The writing itself, of course, is classic Thompson, and thus a wonderful, fun read. And the beautifully reproduced Steadman illustrations are a rare delight in the Thompson library. It's small wonder German art-book publishers Taschen chose to reprint it in coffee-table format. It is a gorgeous example of Steadman's work, but only an adequate one of Thompson's.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15869518530109883982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9281934-louise?shelf=writers">writers</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1006220396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8685593327726243865">I don't know why I have been in Hawaii this long without reading this. It was time to pick it up.<br/><br/>It has all the hallmarks of a Hunter Thompson work.... energy, macho, drugs & alcohol, fast cars, loose talk and some politically uncorrect nouns and adjectives. The drawings of Ralph Steadman add even more edginess. The story is secondary to the style.<br/><br/>Also, like any Hunter Thompson book, there is redeeming content. While an ordinary journalist would cover Waikiki, surfing and flowers... they rate on</span>
  <span id="freeText8685593327726243865" style="display:none">I don't know why I have been in Hawaii this long without reading this. It was time to pick it up.<br /><br />It has all the hallmarks of a Hunter Thompson work.... energy, macho, drugs &amp; alcohol, fast cars, loose talk and some politically uncorrect nouns and adjectives. The drawings of Ralph Steadman add even more edginess. The story is secondary to the style.<br /><br />Also, like any Hunter Thompson book, there is redeeming content. While an ordinary journalist would cover Waikiki, surfing and flowers... they rate only a mention with HST.<br /><br />The first focus is the Honolulu Marathon - not even present in most travel books although it is every bit as international as its NYC and Boston cousins. The description is pure Thompson... and right on.<br /><br />Thompson does the same for 2 week storm on the Big Island - again - not often mentioned in other travel literature.<br /><br />The longest part is about sport fishing in Kona.<br /><br />Interspersed is some period writing of the voyage of Captain Cook and one from Mark Twain.<br /><br />It's a good fast read. I like the layout. Nicely wedged in Hawaiian allusions. Pure, but not vintage, Thompson.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8685593327726243865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10285591408016183009">*Back-dating reviews based on snips I find*<br/><br/>I’m officially taking a break from Hunter S. Thompson. I’m sick of feeling like I’m missing the point. I swear, that blurb has made me paranoid! Every book I read, I’m like “Is it hilarious? Am I missing something hilarious?” I mean, I like his style of writing and I like the fact that much of it surrounds journalism in some form, but I’ve never read a book – barring ‘Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas’ and felt like I really got the tone it was written in,</span>
  <span id="freeText10285591408016183009" style="display:none">*Back-dating reviews based on snips I find*<br /><br />I’m officially taking a break from Hunter S. Thompson. I’m sick of feeling like I’m missing the point. I swear, that blurb has made me paranoid! Every book I read, I’m like “Is it hilarious? Am I missing something hilarious?” I mean, I like his style of writing and I like the fact that much of it surrounds journalism in some form, but I’ve never read a book – barring ‘Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas’ and felt like I really got the tone it was written in, or the jokes – if there was any.<br /><br />I think I’ve gotten myself wound up to the point I’m determined to read more and crack the issue, but it’s just making me more paranoid! That seems about right, considering the excessive drugs in every book of his, like, ever. I’m officially on Hunter hiatus. For now.<br /><br />(I read one of his books a while back and thought it was good - I liked it, but that was all. Then I read the blurb in which a review had called it hilarious. That's almost certainly always going to be the incident I refer to any time I read his books).</span>

  <span id="freeText6799483378884794914" style="display:none">I listened to this on audiobook and so probably didn't catch everything. I found it quite energetic and a lot of fun, with a number of memorable scenes including "trapped on a boat with drug fiends during a midnight storm," "impersonating a Hawaiian deity," and many scenes in which a Samoan war club proves essential. Another possible title could have been Fear and Loathing in Hawaii. I liked this book a lot, and I will definitely read the physical copy at some point in the future. There were a few subplots that never really conclude or develop very far, but the fact that it is mostly based on actual events could have prevented that. If I had to give a ranking right now to the HST novels (or at least non-loosely-organized-essay-collections) that I've read, it would be:<br /><br />1. Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas (amazing)<br />2. Fear and Loathing on the Campaign Trail '72(also amazing)<br />3. The Curse of Lono (pretty great)<br />4. Hell's Angels (some memorable parts, but a bit dry since it predates gonzo)<br />5. The Rum Diary (why did they make this into a movie instead of F&amp;L on the Campaign Trail!?)<br /><br />I think I want to read Better than Sex next, which is supposed to be the 1992 version of F&amp;L on the Campaign Trail from what I've heard.</span>

This is a gigantic book with huge beautiful prints by Steadman as well as the usual crazed drunken rambling of Thompson.<br/><br/>The whole thing is about a trip to Hawaii to cover the Honolulu Marathon that goes awry a la la <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7745.Fear_and_Loathing_in_Las_Vegas_A_Savage_Journey_to_the_Heart_of_the_American_Dream' title='Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas A Savage Journey to the Heart of the American Dream by Hunter S.   <span id="freeText7527075600681848961" style="display:none">I had seen this lying around the local Half-Price Books and snagged it with a coupon diminishing the hefty price tag. This is a gigantic book with huge beautiful prints by Steadman as well as the usual crazed drunken rambling of Thompson.<br /><br />The whole thing is about a trip to Hawaii to cover the Honolulu Marathon that goes awry a la la <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7745.Fear_and_Loathing_in_Las_Vegas_A_Savage_Journey_to_the_Heart_of_the_American_Dream" title="Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas A Savage Journey to the Heart of the American Dream by Hunter S. Thompson" rel="nofollow noopener">Fear and Loathing</a> in Las Vegas and degenerates into a mescaline-fueled marlin hunt with a smattering of interesting pre-American Hawaiian history. The art is great, the writing is fantastic. If you can't justify the $50 tag find a library somewhere that has it. Don't expect to carry the thing on a plane or keep it to read at work or something, it weighs about 5 pounds and is like 18x12.</span>

<div id="review_2462767567" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2462767567" />
    <a title="Jay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79948120-jay"><img alt="Jay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522193277p2/79948120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2462767567?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jay" href="/user/show/79948120-jay">Jay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2462767567" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11368233877997700442">Hunter S. Thompson , on his birthday July 18<br/> Curious and with a grand contempt for all authority, paying attention to the man behind the curtain and bringing us all along with him for the ride, iconoclast and rebel, transgressing boundaries with wild abandon and great joy, an imp of satire, embracing all that is human and moving through all stratum of our society with an ease of blending in and the adaptational skill of a shapeshifter, for whom life is a party and the ordinary becomes spectacula</span>
  <span id="freeText11368233877997700442" style="display:none">Hunter S. Thompson , on his birthday July 18<br /> Curious and with a grand contempt for all authority, paying attention to the man behind the curtain and bringing us all along with him for the ride, iconoclast and rebel, transgressing boundaries with wild abandon and great joy, an imp of satire, embracing all that is human and moving through all stratum of our society with an ease of blending in and the adaptational skill of a shapeshifter, for whom life is a party and the ordinary becomes spectacular pagentry, Hunter S. Thompson is a figure of the Platonic Ideal of an American. <br />In a world full of thou shalt nots, he said yes to life and affirmed the value of other people regardless of our differences.<br /> Notwithstanding his great literary influences Dickens, Hugo, and Flaubert, his stylistics inherited from the Beat and Dadaist-Surrealist writers and the stream of consciousness aesthetic founded by Joyce, we do not read him as we would Stein or Burroughs, as a great and visionary artist who reinvented the possibilities of being human, but as someone in whose adventures we can participate with delight, and in the process learn about ourseelves and our human world.<br /> But there is more. <br /> In his reinvention of news journalism as an inquiry into social issues which is lived by the reader rather than discussed as an essay, the record of an authentic experience of an event and not a dissection of the set of its facts, Hunter S. Thompson created a new art of literature and changed forever the nature of journalism and our expectations of it. He wrote as an avatar of his readers, depriviliging the news as the people's common property and bringing down the wall between author and reader as a revolutionary act. <br /> From his glorious debut book Hell's Angels: A Strange and Terrible Saga, throughout the great and classic works Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas, Fear and Loathing on the Campaign Trail '72, the four volumes of the Gonzo papers (The Great Shark Hunt, Generation of Swine, Songs of the Doomed, Better Than Sex), Fear and Loathing at Rolling Stone, Kingdom of Fear, and the antiimperialist novel The Curse of Lono (an apotheosis of his art which merits study alongside Don Quixote or Finnegan's Wake) , his career may be seen as a disruptive event which both shifted literary aesthetics and radicalized its political and social dimensions.<br /> Read also Hunter S. Thompson: The Last Interview: and Other Conversations , and for context the wonderful illustrations of their adventures together collected as Gonzo: The Art, by Ralph Steadman.<br /> And then go walk among some group of outsiders or crash an event beyond your circle of familiarity, as did he. You never know might happen, or what you might discover, in a life of wandering across boundaries; maybe your own will get broader., as bridges replace walls. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11368233877997700442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2886703582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7317155549201387127">I’m beginning to think I like HST more as a character than as a writer. And even the character, I’m beginning to see as immature. I still have a soft spot for his sarcastic sense of humour, his interminable melodrama and his unflinching macho idealism but I couldn’t really get into this one, and, as douchey as it sounds, at times I find his prose abrasive, undisciplined and hard to read. <br/><br/>For this work, he could have used an assiduous team of interns to help put it all together. <br/>It could have bee</span>
  <span id="freeText7317155549201387127" style="display:none">I’m beginning to think I like HST more as a character than as a writer. And even the character, I’m beginning to see as immature. I still have a soft spot for his sarcastic sense of humour, his interminable melodrama and his unflinching macho idealism but I couldn’t really get into this one, and, as douchey as it sounds, at times I find his prose abrasive, undisciplined and hard to read. <br /><br />For this work, he could have used an assiduous team of interns to help put it all together. <br />It could have been a great novel. It’s not a novel, and it’s not intended to be one, but as it stands, I don't see it as much more than the sum of its disparate parts - short Gonzo blurbs, letters, stuff from other authors, and many Steadman illustrations (which I liked). It seems hastily patched together which is unfortunate given that there was a fun storyline that got off the ground a couple times, only to fold back in on itself. <br />All the material was there, but it seemed like it was rushed out to market or something. And while yes, maybe overall, it did capture the overarching feeling he was shooting for, it could have been “a lot more” in my opinion. There’s no unintended, hidden symbolism here. Everything is just boringly there. <br /><br />I feel it would benefit from more commas and spacing as well. I understand trying to capture the frenetic pace of HST the character, but just because he’s an unhinged cook doesn’t mean the writing should be foresaken and stumble over itself like a drunk.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7317155549201387127" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen K" href="/user/show/2532724-jen-k">Jen K</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2532724-jen-k?shelf=afar-fall-challenge">afar-fall-challenge</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3615793657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8242705833141386662">Despite hiding out and not taking any writing assignments, Hunter S Thompson randomly decides to accept a request to cover the Honolulu Marathon for a running magazine in Oregon. He sets out to Hawaii in December and has convinced his own fiancee and his friend, Ralph, in England to join with his wife and young daughter. Thompson has a few friends and connections in Hawaii and make well intentioned plans to join the marathon race in Honolulu and then vacation in Kono, booking 2 houses on the bea</span>
  <span id="freeText8242705833141386662" style="display:none">Despite hiding out and not taking any writing assignments, Hunter S Thompson randomly decides to accept a request to cover the Honolulu Marathon for a running magazine in Oregon. He sets out to Hawaii in December and has convinced his own fiancee and his friend, Ralph, in England to join with his wife and young daughter. Thompson has a few friends and connections in Hawaii and make well intentioned plans to join the marathon race in Honolulu and then vacation in Kono, booking 2 houses on the beach with plans to fish and relax. The trip starts out somewhat well despite an odd encounter on the flight over and his main contact in Hawaii being a bit of a disreputable man focused on getting drugs, though Ralph struggles early on with a surfing accident. They manage to do some interview with runners and watch bits of the race. Then they head over to Kono and it goes down hill from there with many drugs, alcohol, unexpected storms, flooding and the wild quest for the ultimate marlin. Thompson researched Hawaii by reading a journal about the last voyage of Captain Cook. Cook's time in the islands and the history and culture of the people are interwoven throughout including the legendary god, Lono, which takes an influential toll on Thompson.<br /><br />The story was kind of all over the place and a bit disjointed with some plots unfinished but it was like a car wreck watching all of the bad decisions made and the reactions to them. Thompson definitely has his own methods of travel. There are lots of interesting characters and I hope the innocent managed to get out unscathed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8242705833141386662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2751483681157028492">3.2 stars<br/><br/>This book has not held up well and it’s making me question whether Fear and Loathing was really that funny. <br/><br/>How could I say that? F&L is iconic, classic, loved, and by no one more than myself. Have I changed? The various prejudicial racial pronouncements in Lono don't seem funny. Calling someone a queer doesn't seem funny.   <span id="freeText2751483681157028492" style="display:none">3.2 stars<br /><br />This book has not held up well and it’s making me question whether Fear and Loathing was really that funny. <br /><br />How could I say that? F&amp;L is iconic, classic, loved, and by no one more than myself. Have I changed? The various prejudicial racial pronouncements in Lono don't seem funny. Calling someone a queer doesn't seem funny. Or to someone, I don't really know who. In this one, you sort of know HT is embellishing, I mean well, of course he’s embellishing. But wasn’t he embellishing in F&amp;L too? The whole fishing-trip-drunken-excursion premise seems silly and stupid. <br /><br />Oh well, I don’t know if it’s a case of it's-not-you-it's-me or it's-not-me-but you, you bastard.. Either way, this book fell flat for me. I was bored.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2475150030" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2475150030" />
    <a title="Thomas Fugett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12099850-thomas-fugett"><img alt="Thomas Fugett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588649040p2/12099850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2475150030?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Fugett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Fugett" href="/user/show/12099850-thomas-fugett">Thomas Fugett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2475150030&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2475150030" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6056487249387601340">The Curse of Lono follows the familiar Hunter Thompson formula of: 1. Granted assignment to cover some race/sporting event/political rally. 2. Dutiful covering of event while getting wasted on drugs and alcohol. 3. Procrastination of writing assignment because of continued abuse of drugs and alcohol with the natives. 4. Inevitably being chased out of town after offending natives with boorish, drunken behavior. And then the most important 5th step, exacting revenge on the natives by exposing thei</span>
  <span id="freeText6056487249387601340" style="display:none">The Curse of Lono follows the familiar Hunter Thompson formula of: 1. Granted assignment to cover some race/sporting event/political rally. 2. Dutiful covering of event while getting wasted on drugs and alcohol. 3. Procrastination of writing assignment because of continued abuse of drugs and alcohol with the natives. 4. Inevitably being chased out of town after offending natives with boorish, drunken behavior. And then the most important 5th step, exacting revenge on the natives by exposing their own boorishness and hypocrisy in hilarious, scathing prose. <br /><br />Thompson twists his adventures and his thesis to parallel the adventures of Captain Cook, who was massacred because of the natives perception of him as the returned god Lono. It is a fun and funny read, a bit formulaic as mentioned, but with enough local Hawaiian color to make it original. I didn’t get to see the Steadman illustrations with the online edition I read, but I imagine they would enhance the writing a great deal. Not as good as Fear and Loathing, but richer than the rather dull The Rum Diary, although Lono is much more disjointed and less polished. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6056487249387601340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3551098937?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Thompson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Thompson" href="/user/show/11657479-richard-thompson">Richard Thompson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3551098937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13677838478045674391">Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas is a classic. There is nothing like it. This one is a pale imitation of Fear and Loathing, but it is still pretty damned good. HST takes us on a gonzo joy ride, reporting on the Honolulu Marathon and then going on a wild drug fueled rampage through the Kona Coast with crazy fishing, unbelievable weather and a giant sick dog with deadly red fleas. The story is interspersed with excerpts of the narrative of Captain Cook's death which provide an eerie parallel to Thom</span>
  <span id="freeText13677838478045674391" style="display:none">Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas is a classic. There is nothing like it. This one is a pale imitation of Fear and Loathing, but it is still pretty damned good. HST takes us on a gonzo joy ride, reporting on the Honolulu Marathon and then going on a wild drug fueled rampage through the Kona Coast with crazy fishing, unbelievable weather and a giant sick dog with deadly red fleas. The story is interspersed with excerpts of the narrative of Captain Cook's death which provide an eerie parallel to Thompson's story.<br /><br />It's easy to dismiss Thompson's writing as being lightweight pseudojournalism, but I think that there is some literary quality here. His voice is unique. The literary devices that he uses to achieve his pyrotechnic style are not original, and his writing becomes a bit hard to take in large doses. But the overall effect is unique and original and impossible to read without smiling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13677838478045674391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2418566640" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2418566640" />
    <a title="Dan Furoy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42609850-dan-furoy"><img alt="Dan Furoy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430275861p2/42609850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2418566640?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Furoy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Furoy" href="/user/show/42609850-dan-furoy">Dan Furoy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2418566640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4113383000759598982">A gonzo take on Hawaiian life from the king of bizarre interpretations of popular culture. I can see why this book isn’t for everyone, or even all of HST’s fans, but as someone who has spent a large portion of my life living as a white haole boy in HI, it really rings true. It’s still a drug and alcohol fueled psycho trip, but the allusions to Island culture and folklore, told thru the good doctor’s skewed world view, were equal parts haunting and hilarious. Ralph Steadman’s bizarre illustration</span>
  <span id="freeText4113383000759598982" style="display:none">A gonzo take on Hawaiian life from the king of bizarre interpretations of popular culture. I can see why this book isn’t for everyone, or even all of HST’s fans, but as someone who has spent a large portion of my life living as a white haole boy in HI, it really rings true. It’s still a drug and alcohol fueled psycho trip, but the allusions to Island culture and folklore, told thru the good doctor’s skewed world view, were equal parts haunting and hilarious. Ralph Steadman’s bizarre illustrations are also a major win. If you have spent any decent amount of time in the Islands and also enjoy the works of HST, you have no reason not to enjoy this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4113383000759598982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben Houge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben Houge" href="/user/show/88821262-ben-houge">Ben Houge</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3140913998" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10303953048639767830">Kind of hard for me to relate to, as I might have anticipated, what with all the heroin and mescaline. But some pretty funny bits. Lavish package, some fairly amusing illustrations by Ralph Steadman, and interesting format, interspersing letters and historical documents about Captain Cook among the original writing. Picked it up for the Honolulu Marathon angle, which is really only the jumping off point and not particularly illuminating; after that, things get pretty surreal. But still hope to r</span>
  <span id="freeText10303953048639767830" style="display:none">Kind of hard for me to relate to, as I might have anticipated, what with all the heroin and mescaline. But some pretty funny bits. Lavish package, some fairly amusing illustrations by Ralph Steadman, and interesting format, interspersing letters and historical documents about Captain Cook among the original writing. Picked it up for the Honolulu Marathon angle, which is really only the jumping off point and not particularly illuminating; after that, things get pretty surreal. But still hope to run the Honolulu Marathon someday. Helped with my Hawaii geography. Interesting also as a portrait of US anxiety at the dawn of the 80's.</span>

<div id="review_1793022893" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1793022893" />
    <a title="Amir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36064689-amir"><img alt="Amir" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1793022893?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amir" href="/user/show/36064689-amir">Amir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1793022893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16893885626579098170">This is the kind of story that could make for a compelling movie, but as a book in the manner it's told the story fails to stay interesting. Those who are Hunter S. Thompson devotees will appreciate the prose for its style -- signature HST language and scenes. As far as content goes, though, it's choppy, repetitive, confusing, and too often pretentious. Then again, I was never a fan of Fear and Loathing, so maybe HST just isn't my thing.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="J.J. Ulizio" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72724877-j-j-ulizio"><img alt="J.J. Ulizio" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586176968p2/72724877.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3296496943?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.J. Ulizio" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.J. Ulizio" href="/user/show/72724877-j-j-ulizio">J.J. Ulizio</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553345230" href="/book/show/961502.The_Curse_of_Lono">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3296496943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6770054104053690478">A must for fans of Thompson. But if you are not already a fan of his work I wouldn’t call this a good starting point. Beautifully written about a job that he took to cover a marathon in Hawaii and how it morphed into a fishing adventure in a manner that could only be experienced and written about by HST. Accompanied by the psychedelic artwork from the great Ralph Steadman. A must for any HST fan. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3239131755671776338">Savage in its hilarity, 'The Curse of Lono' is an often overlooked gem of the HST collection.<br/>From superstitious 'natives' to an ocean devoid of fish in a paradise beset by storms, this book has everything, (with the exception of adequate coverage of the Honolulu marathon). A must read for HST fans wanting to broaden their HST vocabulary.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YRSqAt9yjt8O77Zm/XGemNGPu/hzPXA3sB5xcC4Nw5XQm43YmmSgEJVN2VtTVhfzQDgHk/ZZkP2bwmtvFpnepw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6cJUQB2ojBQYXFV4cdsBKl4i8Sejda4WjGUOenNWX3hYTXOaWL6i24P+OkXf/IhBz5VNTCYRTtynuRRlS8JCSg==')})
  }

Thompson\'s crusade against Richard Nixon and the threat of fascism in America--and the devastating price he paid for it Hunter S. Thompson is often misremembered as a wise-cracking, drug-addled cartoon character. This book reclaims him for what he truly was: a fearless opponent of corruption and fascism, one who sacrificed his future well-being to fight against it, rewriting the rules of journalism and political satire in the process. This skillfully told and dramatic story shows how Thompson saw through Richard Nixon\'s treacherous populism and embarked on a life-defining campaign to stop it. In his fevered effort to expose institutional injustice, Thompson pushed himself far beyond his natural limits, sustained by drugs, mania, and little else. For ten years, he cast aside his old ambitions, troubled his family, and likely hastened his own decline, along the way producing some of the best political writing in our history. This timely biography recalls a period of anger and derangement in American politics, and one writer with the guts to tell the truth.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16456668697784725540\" href=\"#\" Burroughs from St. Louis, stabbed a man named David Kammerer with a Boy Scout knife and threw his body in the Hudson River. For eight years, Kammerer had fawned over the younger Carr, but that night something happened: either Carr had had enough or he was forced to defend himself.\n\nThe next day, his clothes stained with blood, Carr went to his friends Bill Burroughs and Jack Kerouac for help. Doing so, he involved them in the crime. A few months later, they were caught up in the crime in a different way. \n\nSomething about the murder captivated the Beats, especially Kerouac and Burroughs, who decided to collaborate on a novel about the events of the previous summer. At the time, the two authors were still unknown, yet to write anything of note. Narrating alternating chapters, they pieced together a hard-boiled tale of bohemian New York during World War II, full of drugs and art, obsession and violence, with scenes and characters drawn from their own lives.\n\nThey submitted their manuscript—called And the Hippos Were Boiled in Their Tanks after an absurd line from a radio bulletin about a circus fire—to publishers, but it was rejected and confined to a filing cabinet for decades. Finally published, at long last, And the Hippos Were Boiled in Their Tanks tells the story of Ramsay Allen and the object of his fixation, the charismatic, idealistic young Phillip Tourian. Phillip and his friends drink and dream in the bars and apartments of the West Village, until, with his friend Mike Ryko (Kerouac\'s narrator), he hatches a plan to ship out as a merchant marine. They\'ll catch a boat for France and jump ship, then make their way through the front to Paris. \n\nAnd the Hippos Were Boiled in Their Tanks is an engaging, fast-paced read that shows the two authors\' developing styles. In this vital investigation, he gathers together the evidence of their full and shocking extent – and how a new reform movement within science is fighting back. Often witty yet deadly serious, Science Fictions is at the vanguard of the insurgency, proposing a host of remedies to save and protect this most valuable of human endeavours from itself.<\/span>\n It is a dark and absorbing example of the Gothic sensibility and bracing satirical voice that are united in Flannery O\'Conner\'s work. In it, the orphaned Francis Marion Tarwater and his cousins, the schoolteacher Rayber, defy the prophecy of their dead uncle--that Tarwater will become a prophet and will baptize Rayber\'s young son, Bishop. A series of struggles ensues: Tarwater fights an internal battle against his innate faith and the voices calling him to be a prophet while Rayber tries to draw Tarwater into a more &quot;reasonable&quot; modern world. Both wrestle with the legacy of their dead relatives and lay claim to Bishop\'s soul.O\'Connor observes all this with an astonishing combination of irony and compassion, humor and pathos, resulting in       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5237.Hunter_S_Thompson">About Hunter S. Thompson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5237.Hunter_S_Thompson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1206560814p3/5237.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5237.Hunter_S_Thompson">Hunter S. Thompson
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
9,802
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6647365942564161243">Hunter Stockton Thompson was an American journalist and author, famous for his book <i>Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas</i>. He is credited as the creator of Gonzo journalism, a style of reporting where reporters involve themselves in the action to such a degree that they become the central figures of their stories. He is also known for his promotion and use of psychedelics and other mind-altering substanc</span>
  <span id="freeText6647365942564161243" style="display:none">Hunter Stockton Thompson was an American journalist and author, famous for his book <i>Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas</i>. He is credited as the creator of Gonzo journalism, a style of reporting where reporters involve themselves in the action to such a degree that they become the central figures of their stories. He is also known for his promotion and use of psychedelics and other mind-altering substances (and to a lesser extent, alcohol and firearms), his libertarian views, and his iconoclastic contempt for authority. He committed suicide in 2005. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6647365942564161243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
